data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20electrolyte%20interphase">cathode electrolyte interphase</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=X-ray+Photoelectron+Spectroscopy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_X-ray%20photoelectron%20spectroscopy">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Impedance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_impedance">impedance</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The commercialization of the rechargeable lithium ion battery (LIB) in the early 1990s <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> by Sony propelled the development of portable electronics. Technologies that once seemed impossible are now ingrained in modern society and have become a part of everyday life. This is largely due to the components within the battery that allow lithium ions to intercalate/deintercalate between the carbon anode and transition metal oxide cathode through the electrolyte, making it a high gravimetric energy density system. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2-4)</a> As consumers become more aware of global climate change, the applications for LIBs are being extended to power hybrid and plug-in electric vehicles; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> however, using LIBs as a ubiquitous energy storage and conversion system could increase the demand for lithium, causing exorbitant prices of Li resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Therefore, researchers have focused their efforts on finding alternative systems that could replace LIBs in specific applications. Sodium (Na) ion battery (NIBs) research was first investigated in the mid 1970s, where the sodium analog to lithium transition metal oxides were first reported. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7, 8)</a> In the recent decade, NIB research has significantly increased given the above-mentioned concerns with LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9, 10)</a> NIBs offer several advantages over its lithium counterpart, given that the demand for sodium is much lower than lithium and that sodium is more abundant. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Overall, the cost of manufacturing NIBs can be lower than that for LIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a>which can be attributed to the ability to use aluminum as the current collector for both the anode and cathode, eliminating the use of Cu, a heavier and more expensive material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Though NIBs will unlikely reach the energy density of LIBs because of sodium’s increased atomic mass and reduced electrochemical potential of 2.71 V (Li = 3.01 V), it could be an alternative for large grid storage applications where cost plays a more significant role. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 and 13">(10, 13)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Within the past decade, several advancements have been made on the anode for NIBs where hard carbon, sodium titanate, tin oxide, and tin sulfide (few of several anode chemistries) have been extensively studied. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14-17)</a> Given that in a full cell the cathode has a higher mass fraction than the anode and operates at a higher potential increasing the capacity in NIBs requires more exploration on high-voltage cathode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Layered transition metal cathode materials (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) are of particular interest because of their high operating voltage, specific capacity, and synthesis yield. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 10, 18 and 19">(10, 18, 19)</a> This class of cathode materials can be classified by their crystal structure stacking: P2, O2, P3, and O3. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The first letters “P” and “O” correspond to where the alkali metal lies within the crystal structure: either in the prismatic or octahedral site. The number is related to the number of repeating Na layers within the unit cell. Of these classes of materials, P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> has been extensively studied due to its high specific capacity (173 mAh/g) and high operating voltage (up to 4.5 V). Lu et al. demonstrated that Na ions can reversibly intercalate and deintercalate using in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), albeit undergoing detrimental phase transformation concurrently. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Lee et al. determined via synchrotron XRD and first-principles calculations that the long voltage plateau above 4.22 V corresponds to a O2 phase transformation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> While the material has a Na content of 1/3 to 2/3, P2 phase is the lowest energy state. However, when the Na content falls to zero then O2 phase becomes the lowest, causing poor capacity retention. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22, 23)</a> Yet, this can be combated by lowering the operating voltage from 4.5 to 4.1 V, eliminating the phase transformation and increasing the rate capability (Capacity at 1C = 85% capacity at C/20). This is not a perfect solution because it sacrifices a large amount of energy by lowering the specific capacity from 174 to 90 mAh/g. Researchers have used doping (Li, Mg, and Zn) to maintain the high operating voltage and capacity, while reducing and/or eliminating the phase transformation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 24 to 27">(24-27)</a> As demonstrated by Wang and co-workers, substituting Mg (<i>x</i> = 0.02–0.15) in place of Ni directly mitigates the P2 to O2 phase transformation when cycled to a cutoff voltage of 4.35 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> However, the cathode operating potential may also cause the electrolyte to decompose forming the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) which may also participate in the capacity degradation. Despite that doping improves the cycling stability of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> class of materials, researchers have failed to address the carbonate based electrolyte instability above 4.2 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Forming a protective layer that allows Na ions to diffuse through the layer and reduce the electrolyte decomposition is one possible way to improve the electrolyte–electrode interface. Atomic layer deposition (ALD) is used in a variety of applications as a coating technique, in particular for battery electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Several advantages in using ALD are as follows: (1) conformal coating, with the ability to coat various irregular materials, (2) controllable coating thickness down to angstroms or monolayers, and (3) apply to a wide range of coating materials (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, ZnO, and HfO<sub>2</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> For this reason, ALD has largely been adopted for coating LIB electrodes and shown to improve cycling performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 32 to 40">(32-40)</a> Considering the success on LIBs, researchers have adopted ALD coating for NIB anode materials. Zhao and co-workers have shown improved cycling performance of disodium terephthalate by Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Recently, Han et al. used ALD to coat Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on tin nanoparticles to elevate volume expansion in the anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Few reports have reported the use of ALD coatings as cathode materials for NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Liu et al. demonstrated the effect of wet chemical Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating on P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode for NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The issues with wet chemistry coating are that it is not able to control the coating thickness, it is difficult to have a conformal coating, and it can introduce contamination during the process. Although there is an improvement in capacity retention with this method, it is challenging to determine what percentage it played in improving the cycling performance when the electrode was only cycled to 4.3 V, which partially avoids the phase transformation (full phase transformation occurs at 4.5 V). Moreover, both coating works mainly focused on the electrochemistry to showcase the effect of ALD and wet chemical coating of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on P2-cathode materials. ALD coatings on P2-cathode materials for NIBs is still in its infancy; there is a need to understand how ALD coatings improve the cycling performance by investigating the electrode–electrolyte interface. Since NIB chemistry behaves differently than LIB chemistry, it is likely that the cathode electrolyte interphase can be composed of different components.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we compare uncoated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NaNiMnO) composite electrode to an ultrathin Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated electrode. Rigorous electrochemical characterization demonstrates the positive effect of ALD coating on the composite electrode. For the first time, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is used to elucidate the factors that influence the chemical composition of the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) which enhances the Coulombic efficiency and cycling performance (from 2.3−4.5 V), by decreasing the impedance of the cycled P2-NaNiMnO cathode with ALD coating.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material was synthesized by coprecipitation method following our previous published work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> A stoichiometric amount of the precursors, Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O and Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, were dissolved in deionized water. The transition metal nitrate solutions were titrated into a stoichiometric NaOH solution using a peristaltic pump at rate of 10 mL/h. The solution was stirred slowly to ensure homogeneity. The coprecipitated solid M(OH)<sub>2</sub> was centrifuged and washed with deionized water (three times). The coprecipitated material was dried in the oven to remove excess water and was ground with a stoichiometric amount of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The material was precalinated at 500 °C for 5 h and calcinated in a pellet form at 900 °C for 14 h in a 50 mL porcelain crucible. Electrodes were made by a slurry containing 80 wt % active material (based on the total mass of the P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composite), 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), and 10 wt % acetylene carbon black in <i>n</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone, then mixing for 1 h to ensure proper material disbursement. The slurry was cast on aluminum foil and dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C. The electrodes were cut into disks with an area of 1.97 cm<sup>2</sup>; afterward, they were pressed with until reaching 1 metric tons. The electrodes have a mass loading of 5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>.</div><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.2 Aluminum Oxide Atomic Layer Deposition</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The electrode casted on aluminum foil was coated with aluminum oxide (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) using ALD (Beneq TFS200). The deposition of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> required the use of trimethylaluminum (TMA) as precursor and water as reactor. The carrier gas was nitrogen in 300 mbar, and the reaction temperature was 150 °C. The deposition rate was 1.1 A per cycle. The coating thickness on the electrodes was controlled through the number of cycles performed. After the electrodes were coated, they were vacuum-dried at 80 °C for 3 h to ensure the removal of water.</div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2 Electrochemical Test</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The uncoated and ALD-coated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes were assembled in 2032 coin cells using a glass fiber GF/F (Whatman) filter separator soaked in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate electrolyte (PC). Battery assembly was carried out in an MBraun glovebox (H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.1 ppm). Galvanostatic discharge and charge at various current densities were performed using an Arbin BT2000 battery cycler. Additionally, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out with 10 mV perturbation and ac frequencies from 0.01 to1 × 10<sup>6</sup> Hz on galvanostatic cycled electrodes at OCV, 1 cycle and 100 cycles. The electrodes were assembled in a three-electrode Swagelok cell, where the active material was the working electrode and Na metal served as the counter and working electrode. Whatman GF/F filter separator soaked in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> PC prevents the working and counter electrode from having direct contact. The three electrode cells were then cycled using above-mentioned conditions. This allows for proper isolation of working electrode impedance. A Solatron 1287 Potentiostat was used to measure the impedance at different states of charge and discharge. After the EIS measurements were taken, an equivalent circuit model was fit to the data to analyze the reactions that took place using Z view software (v. 3.4a, Scribner Associates, Inc.).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3 Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the as-synthesized material and postelectrochemical cycling was characterized by a Philips XL30 environmental scanning electron microscope (ESEM) equipped with an energy dispersive X-ray detector (EDX) operating at 10 kV. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were taken with a FEI 200 kV Sphera Microscope. Samples for TEM were prepared by focused ion beam (FEI Scios DualBeam FIB/SEM), following the procedure from our previous work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Samples were thinned within 100 nm. During FIB thinning process, only 5 kV voltage and 7 pA current were applied to the sample when the sample thickness is within 200 nm. This measure can minimize the beam-induced damaging within only within 10 nm. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> A lift-out procedure with optimized FIB fabrication conditions was conducted on the coated ALD electrode and loaded on the Omni Probe grid (Ted Pella) which is needed to retain the electrochemical activity of the nanobattery. ImageJ was used to determine the ALD coating thickness on the entire electrode.</div><div id="sec2_3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.1 X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy</h4><div class="NLM_p">After electrochemical cycling, the cells were disassembled in the glovebox and washed with DEC to remove excess sodium salt. XPS was performed at the Laboratory for Electron and X-ray Instrumentation using a Kratos AXIS Supra. In order to avoid air exposure, the samples were prepared in the glovebox connected to the XPS. Samples were transferred from the glovebox to the XPS from an nitrogen atmosphere to an ultrahigh vacuum greater than 10<sup>8</sup> Torr. The XPS was operated using Al anode source at 15 kV. All XPS measurements were collected with a 300 μm by 700 μm spot size without using a charge neutralizer during acquisition. Survey scans were collected with a 1.0 eV step size followed by high-resolution scans with a step size of 0.05 eV, for carbon 1s, oxygen 1s, sodium1s, fluorine 1s, nickel 2p (shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), manganese 2p (shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), aluminum 2p, and phosphorus 2p regions.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Fits of the XPS spectra were performed with CasaXPS software (version 2.3.15, Casa Software, Ltd.) to estimate the atomic compositions and chemical species comprising the cathode electrode interphase. All species were fit using a Shirley background. The resulting spectra were then refit, and all spectra were shifted relative to the binding energy of the carbon 1s sp<sup>3</sup> (assigned to 284.8 eV) to compensate for any offset during the measurement.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1 Aluminum Oxide ALD Coating Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by ALD cathode coatings on LIB cathode materials, we coated classical P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2-NaNiMnO) cathode with aluminum oxide (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) by ALD. The phase pure crystalline P2-NaNiMnO material is validated by the rietveld refinement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). It is widely accepted that both binder and conductive additive contribute to the surface reactions caused by the instability of the electrolyte at high voltages. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> To combat these issues, ALD was used to coat Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on the P2-NaNiMnO composite electrode surface. The deposition temperature occurred at 150 °C to prevent the chemical decomposition of the PVDF binder, ensuring that a stable electrode is used. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> shows the SEM images of the as synthesized electrode material and the ALD-coated electrode. EDS mapping was used to show that Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating is uniform throughout the electrode and that it did not affect the electrode morphology. Since the entire electrode was coated, it is important to distinguish the amorphous ALD coating from the amorphous conductive carbon. First, a section of the electrode was cut using focused-ion beam (FIB) to allow proper characterization of the electrode. If FIB is not used and the coating thickness is characterized by scrapping the electrode and loading the material on the TEM grid for imaging, then one is unable to distinguish the amorphous material, leading to improper ALD thickness characterization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows a uniform ALD coating of 1 nm on the P2-NaNiMnO particle. The higher resolution TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) gives a detailed view of the amorphous layer to the crystalline active material and a uniform ALD coating. The particle has a <i>d</i> spacing of 1.86 Å, equivalent to the (104) plane as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. TEM images of the uncycled Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composite electrode. (a) Low magnification image of coated pristine particle and (b) high magnification image determining the coating thickness of approximately 1 nm.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2 Galvanostatic Cycling Comparison of Uncoated and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">The uncoated and ALD-coated P2-NaNiMnO electrodes were assembled into coin cells, then cycled in galvanostatic mode at C/20 rate. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a highlights the materials first cycle voltage profiles, demonstrating the quintessential plateaus for this material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref48" aria-label="reference 22 and 48">(22, 48)</a> The Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated ALD electrode (blue), however, shows slight increase in the discharge capacity of 142.6 mAh/g compared to the uncoated electrode (134.6 mAh/g). More importantly, the P2–O2 phase transformation is evident in both electrodes in the charge state, demonstrating that ALD coating does not eliminate such a structure transformation. The differential capacity versus voltage profiles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) shows five distinct d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> peaks that correspond to the intercalation of Na-ions. The sharp peak at 4.20 V is due to the P2–O2 phase transformation associated with this class of material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> This peak is less intense in the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode, owing to the strong binding of the coating to the surface of the material and thus slightly reducing the phase transformation and possibly forming the CEI. The coating does not eliminate the phase transformation but may aid in maintaining the particle integrity. Furthermore, a small peak at 2.98 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b insert) appears in the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots of the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode, which is not shown for the uncoated sample in this work nor in the literature, it might be possible that this could be from the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reacting during electrochemical cycling.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Voltage profiles and their corresponding differential voltage plots of uncoated and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated electrodes at (a, b) cycle 1 and (c, d) cycle 50.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After repeated cycling, the material structure degrades as a result of the severe phase transformation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). The ALD-coated electrode still retains more of the structure properties at the 50th cycle as demonstrated by the voltage plateaus. As shown, the coated electrode has a higher capacity of 92.3 mAh/g compared to the uncoated electrode capacity of 69.7 mAh/g. Given that the material has gone under repeated phase transformation, the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> of the 50th cycle depicts that the peaks are no longer as sharp as they were in the first cycle. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, the peak at 2.98 V persists after 50 cycles. Finally, it is obvious that Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating reduces the overall potential in the cell throughout electrochemical cycling. This is a common occurrence in Li-ion cathode electrodes when coated with an ALD type coating. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49, 50)</a> Though bulk Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is insulating in nature, the ultrathin coating and amorphous nature reduce the insulating effects at lower current density, allowing the transport of Na-ions through the film, yet its effects are still observed.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical characterization of the uncoated and ALD-coated P2-NaNiMnO electrodes are provided in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. The theoretical capacity of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO cathode material is 173 mAh/g due to the Ni redox reaction from Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>4+</sup> corresponding to the mole ratio of the Na ions. Given that the coating is 1 nm, which attributes to less than one percent of the total material weight, therefore, the coating weight is negligible. Both electrodes were cycled at constant current from 4.5 to 2.3 V for 100 cycles. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a demonstrates the capacity versus cycle plot for the ALD-coated and uncoated electrodes. The ALD-coated electrode improves the capacity retention of P2-NaNiMnO. After 100 cycles, the cell exhibits a capacity of 77.43 mAh/g, while the uncoated electrode has a capacity of 52 mAh/g. As expected the thin Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating did not bring a drastic improvement in capacity retention because of the severe phase transformation that occurs above 4.2 V. However, the coating does improve the capacity retention and may aid in other aspects of the electrochemical performance. By observing the value of the <i>x</i>-axis of each half cycle (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,c), one can estimate the storage capacity of each electrode. Ultimately, the ratio of the two capacities is known as the Coulombic efficiency (CE). This is one way to quantify the irreversibility of each cycle, shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. We note that the thin coating improves the CE of the active material throughout electrochemical cycling. The first cycle CE for the ALD-coated electrode is 91.6% and quickly reaches 99% in the fifth cycle. Conversely, the uncoated electrode exhibits a first cycle efficiency of 83.8 and 95.2% CE by the fifth cycle. Throughout electrochemical cycling, the uncoated electrode has an unstable CE as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The CE fluctuates significantly while the ALD-coated P2-NaNiMnO electrode maintains a stable CE, demonstrated by the flat curve. Comparing our previous work with the ALD-coated LIB cathodes, we present similar effects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 50 to 53">(50-53)</a> Wise et al. demonstrated that an ultrathin coating of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> can significantly improve the electrode–electrolyte interface, reducing the decomposition of the electrolyte on high voltage cathode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The addition of the artificial Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> CEI coating on P2-NaNiMnO active material enhances the interface by reducing the exposure of the electrolyte to the active material. The 1 nm Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating may help reduce the decomposition of the electrolyte by protecting the active material, PVDF binder, and conductive carbon from reacting with the electrolyte; therefore, improving the CE throughout electrochemical cycling.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Galvanostatically cycled electrodes demonstrating (a) specific capacity versus cycle number at C/20 rate and Coulombic efficiency as a function of cycle number and (b) rate capability plot for Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated (blue) and uncoated (red).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical rate performance test is a good way to measure the kinetic property of the material at various charge and discharge rates. Here, the material is put under various stresses implemented through incremental current increase every few cycles. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b compares the rate capability of the uncoated P2-NaNiMnO and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated electrode. The rate performance begins at C/20, increases to 1C then returns back to C/20 after a 25 cycles period. The uncoated P2-NaNiMnO electrode exhibits inferior rate capabilities when increasing the rate from C/20 to C/10 and then to C/5 and finally to C/2 compared to the ALD-coated electrode. The disparity capacity increases at C/2, where the coated electrode has an average specific capacity of 105.6 mAh/g compared to 78.5 mAh/g for the uncoated electrode. Thus, far, the coated electrode has outperformed the uncoated P2-NaNiMnO. However, at 1C the ALD electrode retains less capacity. Comparing our work to ALD coatings on the cathode materials for LIBs, the effect of thin Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coatings on high voltage LIB cathode materials differ significantly. In some cases, the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> substantially improves the rate capability by preventing transition metal dissolution and reducing electrolyte decomposition. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref52 ref53 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref52 ref53 ref55" aria-label="reference 50, 52, 53 and 55">(50, 52, 53, 55)</a> Conversely, there are accounts that demonstrate the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating has lower capacity than the bare electrode at higher rates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> These effects are not ubiquitous with one type of active material; the high-voltage lithium nickel manganese oxide, for example, has poor rate capability when coated with Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Riley et al. clearly show that at higher rates from C/4 to 1C the ALD-coated electrode has a capacity lower than that of the bare Li(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub> Co<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>, which is attributed to the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> creating a barrier for ion mobility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Although it is difficult to compare Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating on various cathode materials in LIB due to electrode configuration, the above-mentioned cases can be applied to NIB coated cathode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p last">There are few works that have used Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as a protective layer for SIB cathode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43, 44)</a> Kaliyappan and co-workers investigated a series of ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrodes with discrete thicknesses on P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Mn<sub>0.54</sub> Ni<sub>0.13</sub>Co<sub>0.13</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> and the effects on rate performance. They determined that a thinner coating outperforms the bare cathode while a thicker coating hinders the rate capabilities due to the insulating properties of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> However, when we compare our rate performance results with the work of Kaliyappan and co-workers, our work does not follow the same trend, i.e., a thin coating improves the capacity retention at high currents. However, it is commonly agreed upon that P2 cathodes suffer from severe phase transformations and that doping cobalt into the P2-NaNiMnO cathode enhances the cycling performance. Therefore, the improvement in rate capability for thin ALD coating in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Mn<sub>0.54</sub> Ni<sub>0.13</sub>Co<sub>0.13</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> is not only due to the coating effect but also the improved stability as a result of cobalt doping. Our material demonstrated the true effect of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating in a traditional P2-NaNiMnO without doping. Computation techniques are widely used to help validate experimental work or understand fundamental mechanisms that occur within a battery system during electrochemical cycling. Jung et al. used ab initio molecular dynamics calculations to investigate the sodiation through Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and compare it to Li ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> They conclude that Na-ion diffusion occurs much faster through Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> compared to Li ions even though the Na ions are much larger, albeit the study was conducted through a crystalline Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. However, the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in our case is amorphous where we see that at fast rate (1C) the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode has lower rate capability than the uncoated electrode, demonstrating that the diffusivity is subpar in the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> film.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.3 Cathode Interfacial Resistance</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the changes in the impedance spectra of the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated and uncoated P2-NaNiMnO electrodes cycled galvanostatically in a three electrode Swagelok cell. Using a three-electrode configuration allows us to hone in on the working electrode (P2-NaNiMnO cathode) impedance while eliminating the effects associated from the reference and working electrode (Na metal), as demonstrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Previous reports failed to investigate the effect of the ALD coating on the cathode. They took into account the impedance of the sodium metal in addition to the coating which could lead to inaccurate impedance quantification. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43, 44)</a> Furthermore, there is a need to understand the effect of the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b demonstrates the Nyquist plots for electrodes in the charged state after 1 and 100 cycles. The Nyquist plots depicts the measured real versus imaginary impedance over a series of ac frequencies. We can quantitatively analyze the impedance spectra by a model circuit generated by several reactions that occur in the cell during electrochemical cycling (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). The model accounts for the ohmic resistance of the electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>Ω</sub>), the double layer capacitance of the electrode/electrolyte interface (CPE<sub>sf</sub>), resistance due to the sodium ion diffusion through the surface reactions on the cathode (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>), the double-layer capacitance (CPE<sub>dl</sub>), and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 50, 51, 59 and 60">(50, 51, 59, 60)</a> Finally, the model accounts for the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) known as the impedance according to solid state diffusion of the Na ion through the bulk of the active material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Nyquist plots of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO cycled electrodes (red) and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated cycled electrodes (blue) and uncoated (blue), at (a) cycle 1 and (b) cycle 100. The data was fit based on the circuit shown in (c).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Comparing the uncoated and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrodes after the first cycle (cycled at C/20), both the <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> are significantly lower in the coated electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). After 1 cycle the uncoated P2-NaNiMnO has a surface film resistance of 878.8Ω while the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated P2-NaNiMnO has an <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> value of 182Ω. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> associated with the Na ion diffusion through the electrode-CEI interface is 3948Ω for the uncoated electrode compared to 420Ω for the coated electrode. After 100 cycles (cycled at C/20), the <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> increased significantly for the uncoated electrode compared to the coated electrode. The ultrathin coating protects the surface of the electrode, reducing the effects that the binder and conductive additive have on electrolyte decomposition. Though Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is insulating in nature, the thin coating allows for Na ions to diffuse through the coating film. Given that the surface film resistance increases slightly from 1 to 100 cycles, the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating reduces the electrolyte decomposition at high potentials, forming a better CEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref56" aria-label="reference 38 and 56">(38, 56)</a> From <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, the charge transfer resistance increases significantly compared to the coated P2-NaNiMnO. This implies that the active material structure has degraded significantly during electrochemical cycling, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. Therefore, the ALD coating helps suppressing the structure instabilities associated with sodiation and desodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> To validate the effect of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating, postcycling characterization is required.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Impedance Measurement Values for Coated and Uncoated Cycled Electrodes</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">1300</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.4 Ex Situ Electrode Characterization</h3><div id="sec3_4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.4.1 Scanning Electron Microscopy</h4><div class="NLM_p">The above-mentioned electrochemistry has clearly demonstrated that the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated electrode improves the cycling performance, CE, the surface film resistance, and charge transfer resistance. Part of the improvements can be attributed to particle stability; to examine this, SEM images were taken after 100 cycles at C/20 rate. The pristine electrode clearly shows the P2-NaNiMnO active material surrounded by acetylene black (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). After repeated slow cycling, the active material endures repeated sodiation and desodiation. As the Na ions are extracted from the P2-NaNiMnO structure, the material compensates by the restructuring of the metal–oxide layer, causing the oxygen layer to glide to reduce the steric hindrance within the crystal structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref22 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref22 ref61" aria-label="reference 19, 22 and 61">(19, 22, 61)</a> Lu et al. proposed stacking faults occur as a result of the shift in oxygen–oxygen contact due to the instability of the metal oxide layer during sodium extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> Therefore, it is not surprising that the cycled uncoated P2-NaNiMnO demonstrated severe particle exfoliation. This is consistent with work done by Liu et al., where they investigated the failure mechanism of P2-NaNiMnO after 300 cycles at 1C using SEM and TEM. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Although this material undergoes an intercalation reaction mechanism, it can still exhibit stress and strain within the particle during electrochemical cycling. This is evident in our previous XRD study of the material: When the sodium concentration is less than 1/3, the oxygen layers are in direct contact leading to an electrostatic repulsion which directly expands the <i>c</i> parameter. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Conversely, the ALD-coated electrode significantly reduces particle exfoliation after 100 cycles, and only few particles showed signs of particle degradation. The notion that ALD coating can be used to maintain particle integrity has been demonstrated in alloying anodes (Si and Sn) for LIBs by holding the particle together after 300% volume expansion during repeated lithiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 35, 63 and 64">(35, 63, 64)</a> In their case, a thick ALD coating can improve the integrity of the active material without forming a strong binding interaction between the coating and the active material. Since we applied an ultrathin coating, it is speculated that the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating forms Na–Al–O bonds to form a strong binding interaction that can reduce particle exfoliation. The detailed mechanism still requires more in-depth analysis. Having the ability to maintain electrode stability can be traced to the charge transfer resistance (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>), which is the resistance associated with Na ion diffusion through the surface of the active material through the SEI. To further understand how Na ion kinetics is affected by ALD coating, it is needed to further investigate the effects of electrolyte decomposition on the P2-NaNiMnO particle surface both coated and uncoated.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. SEM images of (a) pristine uncoated and uncycled Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode, cycled electrodes after 100 cycles (b) uncoated and (c) Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.4.2 Cathode Electrolyte Interphase Investigation by XPS</h4><div class="NLM_p">The surface of each electrode was analyzed before cycling (uncycled, not exposed to electrolyte) in addition to after the first charge to 4.1 and 4.5 V and 5 and 100 cycles, avoiding air exposure as described in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>. The elemental atomic percentage of the cycled electrodes is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>, where we can see the elemental evolution of the SEI throughout electrochemical cycling. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> demonstrates that the cycled electrodes are largely dominated by acetylene black at 284.4 eV. Before cycling, the electrodes have the signature peaks of the PVDF polymer binder at 285.5 eV (CH<sub>2</sub>), 290.5 eV, and 292.7 eV (CF<sub>2</sub>). Once the electrodes are cycled to 4.1 V, the decomposition products of the PC electrolyte begin to evolve, forming CO, OCO, and CO<sub>3</sub> moieties for both uncoated and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated. After the electrodes are cycled to 4.5 V, the PVDF peak corresponding to C–F<sub>2</sub> broadens due to the formation of sodium carbonate species. Prolonged cycling causes all peaks to shift to higher binding energy and form more CO components in the in the coated electrode, which is largely associated with ethers, esters, and oligomeric species of poly(ethylene oxide) (CH<sub>2</sub>–CH<sub>2</sub>–O)<sub><i>n</i></sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> from the PC electrolyte decomposition. These functionalities can also be seen in the O 1s spectra (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>). For the uncoated P2-NaNiMNO electrode, peaks indicative of lattice oxygen (529.3 eV), surface oxygen (531.2 eV), CO from the interaction between the conductive additive and active material (532 eV), and Na auger (536 eV) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> are clearly demonstrated. In the coated electrode, the Al–O (531.9 eV) peak corresponding to Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating as well as all of the above-mentioned peaks being shifted slightly to higher binding energy, due to the interaction with the coating (lattice oxygen at 529.6 eV, surface oxygen at 531.6 eV, CO at 532.6, and Na auger at 536.9 eV). As the electrolyte begins to decompose at 4.1 and 4.5 V, we begin to see organic decomposition products in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a> as well as oxidative species that result from the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub>O<sub><i>z</i></sub> around 534 eV) for both coated and uncoated electrodes. However, for the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode, the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub>O<sub><i>z</i></sub> peak broadens and increases throughout electrochemical cycling. Moreover, the Al–O peak associated with the coating is present even after 100 cycles as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. Both the O 1s spectra and Al 2p spectra show a shift to higher binding energy as the cycle number increases. This could be due to the strong binding interaction that the ALD coating has with the electrode influencing the formation of a different CEI with respect to the uncoated electrode, consistent with the shift in binding energies of the CEI functionalities and an increase in salt decomposition products. Furthermore, this demonstrates the robustness of the coating, also shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, where the ALD-coated electrode preserves the active material integrity even after repeated cycling. Consistent with the literature, we see that as we continue to cycle the electrodes the surface oxygen peak decreases indicative of a thicker CEI formation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> Although it seems that the coated electrode has more CEI formation, the atomic percentage of carbon and oxygen in the surface is less than that of the uncoated P2-NaNiMnO (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>). The dashed line in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> guides the peak shift that occurs throughout electrochemical cycling, which is more prevalent in the coated electrode. When peaks shift to higher binding energies, it signifies that the binding environment is more electronegative which can be further investigated by F 1s (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a), P 2p (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b), and Na 1s (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig10" aria-label="Figure 10">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Elemental atomic percentage of the uncoated and ALD-coated cycled electrodes at first charge 4.1 and 4.5 V and 5 and 100 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. XPS C 1s regions of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO (left) and ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated (right) electrodes cycled to first charge 4.1 and 4.5 V and 5 and 100 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. XPS O 1s regions of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO (left) and ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated (right) electrodes cycled.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) XPS F 1s regions of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO (left) and ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated (right) electrodes cycled and (b) XPS P 2p region of ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated at to first charge 4.1 and 4.5 V and 5 and 100 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/medium/am-2017-05326p_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. XPS Na 1s regions of uncoated P2-NaNiMnO (left) and ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated (right) electrodes cycled to first charge 4.1 and 4.5 V and 5 and 100 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/large/am-2017-05326p_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b05326&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Given that the electrode is composed of 80% active material, 10% PVDF binder, and 10% acetylene black, the uncycled electrodes have a large peak corresponding to the C–F bond of the binder (F 1s at 687 eV). This peak persists throughout cycling and continues to dominate the signal. Consistent with both the F 1s and Na 1s, a NaF peak at 684 eV for the uncoated P2-NaNiMnO and 685 eV for the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode are observed. This is due to the interaction between the active material and the PVDF binder. The Na surface reacts with the PVDF to form NaF from the dehydroflurination, generating HF and reacting with Na, similar to the LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67 ref68 ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67 ref68 ref69" aria-label="reference 67 to 69">(67-69)</a> This is more prevalent in the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coated electrode because it is needed to use the water as a precursor to form the aluminum oxide coating, which causes the PVDF binder to react with water forming more HF. Eventually, the formation of more NaF in the uncycled coated electrode maybe one of the causes for prepping the electrode surface to promote the formation of a more inorganic CEI leading to higher initial CE and overall cycling performance compared to the uncoated electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">As we charge the electrodes to 4.1 V, we begin to see the decomposition products form that result from the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt. Similar to LiPF<sub>6</sub>, the sodium salt is susceptible to a similar process (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>)<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/am-2017-05326p_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/am-2017-05326p_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/asset/images/am-2017-05326p_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span>Contrary to the uncoated electrode, the ALD-coated cycled electrode has an increase in NaF throughout prolonged cycling, exhibited in both <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figures 9">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>. NaF on its own is a highly resistive material and it can be assumed that this would hinder the CEI; however, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. indicate otherwise. As salt decomposes, it is likely that we are not generating a consistent NaF film but rather discrete crystallites that allow for Na ions to pass as seen in the case of LIB. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65 ref70'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65 ref70" aria-label="reference 65 and 70">(65, 70)</a> At 4.1 V, we begin to see the decomposition of the salt (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b), which is largely dominated by Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub>O<sub><i>z</i></sub> moieties generated when sodium continues to react with the OPF products in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>. Little Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub>6</sub> is found when the electrodes are charged to 4.1 and 4.5 V; however, it was observed that the amount of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub>6</sub> increases when the electrodes are cycled both 5 and 100 times. This is largely seen in the uncoated electrode which maybe one of the factors that increases the impedance of the electrode surface, as seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The CEI generated from the coated electrodes is more inorganic causing the electrolyte decomposition functionalities to shift to a higher binding energy. Moreover, it is widely accepted that coating cathode materials for LIBs improves the stability of the electrode–electrolyte interface. In our case, it is likely that the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD coating tends to enhance the interface by protecting the electrode from HF formation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref71'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref71" aria-label="reference 71">(71)</a> Therefore, the electrode is less likely to form less unwanted byproducts as a result, boosting the Coulombic efficiency.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The comparison of a P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode and Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ALD-coated P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>electrode was presented. The ALD coating drastically improved the initial and overall Coulombic efficiency and cathode resistivity. The cathode electrolyte interphase was investigated by XPS, which determined that each electrode generated a different surface film. The uncoated electrode contained more organic species such as carbonates, esters, and alkoxyl functionalities, retained more residual salt, and formed less NaF. Conversely, the coated electrode forms large CO moieties that are associated with polymeric species such as poly(ethylene oxide), which play a critical factor in forming a more flexible CEI that can reduce the exfoliation of the P2-NaNiMnO particle (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). Furthermore, the coated electrode forms more NaF throughout cycling, which plays a vital role in the overall CEI. The CEI generated on the coated P2-NaNiMnO electrode enhances the Na ion kinetics from the bulk of the material through the electrode film versus the uncoated electrode. We demonstrated that optimizing the P2-NaNiMnO surface is a vital parameter in improving the electrode-electrolyte interface which facilitates the cycling performance in this class of materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b05326" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b05326</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of pristine P2-NaNiMnO with its corresponding Rietveld refinement results. SEM/EDX images of the coated and uncoated P2-NaNiMnO cathode (FS2). Focused ion beam sample preparation to determine the ALD coating thickness. EIS of the three electrode cell showing that the cathode impedance can effectively be isolated. XPS spectra of Al 2p, Ni 2p, and Mn 2p. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf">am7b05326_si_001.pdf (956.32 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b05326" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
