        </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of depositing ultrathin (&lt;1 nm) Al2O3 coatings on LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) particles using at. layer deposition (ALD) are presented.  Promising electrochem. performance of the Al2O3 ALD coated LNMO at 30° is demonstrated in not only significantly improved coulombic efficiency, cycle retention, and rate capability, but also in dramatically suppressed self-discharge and dissoln. of transition metals.  Combined analyses by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, ex-situ XPS, and ex-situ time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry reveal that the solid electrolyte interphase layer on the Al2O3 ALD coated LNMO is much thinner and contains fewer org. species than the one on the bare LNMO.  This difference originates from the suppression of the side reaction at high voltage by the Al2O3 ALD protective coating.  Also, fluorination of Al2O3 ALD layer upon repeated charge-discharge cycling is confirmed, and this can account for the capacity increases during the initial charge-discharge cycles.  Finally, it is also demonstrated that a full LNMO/Li4Ti5O12 battery incorporating the Al2O3 ALD coated LNMO as cathode outperforms the one incorporating only bare LNMO.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKxV6OBhXEp7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhW0FRDXUgOcA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvVKltbjN&amp;md5=8b5b69452093194d848867ee1f9e839a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b05500"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhuo, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, F.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhancing the High-Voltage Cycling Performance of LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by Retarding Its Interfacial Reaction with an Electrolyte by Atomic-Layer-Deposited Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancing the High-Voltage Cycling Performance of LiNi0.5Mn0.3Co0.2O2 by Retarding Its Interfacial Reaction with an Electrolyte by Atomic-Layer-Deposited Al2O3</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Yantao; Cui, Suihan; Zhuo, Zengqing; Yang, Wanli; Wang, Xinwei; Pan, Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-voltage (&gt;4.3 V) operation of LiNixMnyCozO2 (NMC; 0 ≤ x, y, z &lt; 1) for high capacity has become a new challenge for next-generation Li-ion batteries because of the rapid capacity degrdn. over cycling.  The authors study the performance of LiNi0.5Mn0.3Co0.2O2 (NMC532) electrodes with and without an at.-layer-deposited (ALD) Al2O3 layer for charging/discharging at 3.0-4.5 V (high voltage).  The results of the electrochem. measurements show that the cells with ALD Al2O3-coated NMC532 electrodes have much enhanced cycling stability.  The mechanism was studied by using XPS, x-ray absorption spectroscopy, and electrochem. methods.  The ultrathin ALD Al2O3 film can reduce the interface resistance of Li-ion diffusion and enhance the surface stability of NMC532 by retarding the reactions at NMC532/electrolyte interfaces for preventing the formation of a new microstructure rock-salt phase NiO around the NMC532 surfaces.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolw8gMKD3ZJrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5E71a5lDwgg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslWjsb%252FP&amp;md5=4166c79e17d880f6bcd076ccbb7b6115</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1021/cm500512n"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, J.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrathin Lithium-Ion Conducting Coatings for Increased Interfacial Stability in High Voltage Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Joong Sun; Meng, Xiangbo; Elam, Jeffrey W.; Hao, Shiqiang; Wolverton, Christopher; Kim, Chunjoong; Cabana, Jordi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3128-3134</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin conformal coatings of the Li ion conductor, Li Al oxide (LiAlO2), were evaluated for their ability to improve the electrochem. stability of LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/graphite Li-ion batteries.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy confirmed the ion conducting character of the LiAlO2 films.  Complementary simulations of the activation barriers in these layers match exptl. results very well.  LiAlO2 films were subsequently sep. deposited onto LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 and graphite electrodes.  Increased electrochem. stability was obsd., esp. in the full cells, which was attributed to the role of the coatings as phys. barriers against side reactions at the electrode-electrolyte interface.  By comparing data from full cells where the coatings were applied to either electrode, the dominating failure mechanism is the diffusion of transition metal ions from the cathode to the anode.  The LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/graphite full cell with &lt;1 nm LiAlO2 on the pos. electrode exhibited a discharge capacity of 92 mA-h g-1 at C/3 rate.  The chem. underpinnings of stable performance were revealed by soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy.  First, both Mn and Ni were detected on the graphite electrode surfaces, and their oxidn. states were detd. as +2.  Second, the ultrathin coatings on the anode alone are sufficient to significantly reduce this deleterious process.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrTozvCXOPDI7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5E71a5lDwgg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXmvFeqtr4%253D&amp;md5=ebafa35fedc3b76295ecc31a84984de5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b02952"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wise, A.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding the Improvement in the Electrochemical Properties of Surface Modified 5 V LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 Spinel Cathodes in Lithium-ion Cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1695-1707</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The 5 V spinel cathode LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 with cation disorder in the 16d octahedral sites has been surface modified with 2 wt.% nanosize Al2O3, ZnO, Bi2O3, and AlPO4 by an electrostatic self-assembly method.  The bare and surface-modified samples have been characterized by x-ray diffraction, FTIR spectroscopy, high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, charge-discharge measurements in lithium batteries, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and XPS.  The surface-modified samples exhibit better cycling performance, better rate capability, and better rate capability retention during cycling compared to the bare sample.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy and XPS studies show that the inferior electrochem. performances of the bare LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 are closely related to the formation of thick solid-electrolyte interfacial (SEI) layer at the high operating voltages of ∼5 V.  Surface modifications with nanosize Al2O3, ZnO, Bi2O3, and AlPO4 suppress the formation of thick SEI layers on LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 and thereby improve the electrochem. performances significantly.  Moreover, the differences in the surface compns. formed during the annealing or electrochem. cycling processes also influence the electrochem. properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVoi9qtM6_n7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5E71a5lDwgg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXjslWmtb4%253D&amp;md5=1bf7a163740043e6ed54a681306e1155</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2010.11.124"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Riley, L.                         cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical effects of ALD surface modification on combustion synthesized LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 as a layered-cathode material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Riley, Leah A.; Van Atta, Sky; Cavanagh, Andrew S.; Yan, Yanfa; George, Steven M.; Liu, Ping; Dillon, Anne C.; Lee, Se-Hee</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Combustion synthesized Li(Ni1/3Mn1/3Co1/3)O2 particles are coated with thin, conformal layers of Al2O3 by at. layer deposition (ALD).  XRD, Raman, and FTIR are used to confirm that no change to the bulk, local structure occurs after coating.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results indicate that the surface of the Li(Ni1/3Mn1/3Co1/3)O2 are protected from dissoln. and HF attack after only 4-layers, or ∼8.8 Å of alumina.  Electrochem. performance at an upper cutoff of 4.5 V is greatly enhanced after the growth of Al2O3 surface film.  Capacity retention is increased from 65% to 91% after 100 cycles at a rate of C/2 with the addn. of only two at. layers.  Due to the conformal coating, the effects on Li(Ni1/3Mn1/3Co1/3)O2 overpotential and capacity are negligible below six ALD-layers.  We propose that the use of ALD for coating on Li(Ni1/3Mn1/3Co1/3)O2 particles makes the material a stronger replacement candidate for LiCoO2 as a pos. electrode in lithium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSJtbukceywbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5E71a5lDwgg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXovFCrug%253D%253D&amp;md5=3fad232cd3ee0e22dda01b27c2382414</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1021/nl503169v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jung, S.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Diffusion in Al2O3: A Distinct Perspective Compared with Lithium Ion Diffusion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jung, Sung Chul; Kim, Hyung-Jin; Choi, Jang Wook; Han, Young-Kyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6559-6563</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Surface coating of active materials has been one of the most effective strategies to mitigate undesirable side reactions and thereby improve the overall battery performance.  In this direction, aluminum oxide (Al2O3) is one of the most widely adopted coating materials due to its easy synthesis and low material cost.  Nevertheless, the effect of Al2O3 coating on carrier ion diffusion has been investigated mainly for Li ion batteries, and the corresponding understanding for emerging Na ion batteries is currently missing.  Using ab initio mol. dynamics calcns., herein, we first find that, unlike lithiation, sodiation of Al2O3 is thermodynamically unfavorable.  Nonetheless, there can still exist a threshold in the Na ion content in Al2O3 before further diffusion into the adjacent active material, delivering a new insight that both thermodn. and kinetics should be taken into account to describe ionic diffusion in any material media.  Furthermore, Na ion diffusivity in NaxAl2O3 turns out to be much higher than Li ion diffusivity in LixAl2O3, a result opposite to the conventional stereotype based on the at. radius consideration.  While hopping between the O-rich trapping sites via an Na-O bond breaking/making process is identified as the main Na ion diffusion mechanism, the weaker Na-O bond strength than the Li-O counterpart turns out to be the origin of the superior diffusivity of Na ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGppsVF5CuqZkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5E71a5lDwgg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslSkurzO&amp;md5=f9cd99fb338931129cb20320c9291c80</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1149/2.100202jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ender, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Weber, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ivers-Tiffée, E.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Analysis of Three-Electrode Setups for AC-Impedance Measurements on Lithium-Ion Cells by FEM Simulations</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Effect of Fluoroethylene Carbonate as an Additive on the Solid Electrolyte Interphase on Silicon Lithium-Ion Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schroder, Kjell; Alvarado, Judith; Yersak, Thomas A.; Li, Juchuan; Dudney, Nancy; Webb, Lauren J.; Meng, Ying Shirley; Stevenson, Keith J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5531-5542</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) has become a std. electrolyte additive for use with Si neg. electrodes, but how FEC affects solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation on the Si anode's surface is still not well understood.  Herein, SEI formed from LiPF6-based carbonate electrolytes, with and without FEC, were studied on 50 nm thick amorphous Si thin film electrodes to understand the role of FEC on Si electrode surface reactions.  In contrast to previous work, anhyd. and anoxic techniques were used to prevent air and moisture contamination of prepd. SEI films.  This allowed for accurate characterization of the SEI structure and compn. by XPS and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry depth profiling.  FEC redn. leads to F- ion and LiF formation, consistent with previous computational and exptl. results.  Surprisingly, also these species decrease Li-ion soly. and increase the reactivity of the Si surface.  The effectiveness of FEC at improving the Coulombic efficiency and capacity retention is due to F- ion formation from redn. of the electrolyte, which leads to the chem. attack of any Si-oxide surface passivation layers and the formation of a kinetically stable SEI comprising predominately LiF and Li oxide.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSv3p3Tp69f7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPEu1PQqbtAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1OqsbzJ&amp;md5=e86fa14fac83156dfafd6d6eed502ba8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.01.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, C.                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Surface chemistry and morphology of the solid electrolyte interphase on silicon nanowire lithium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chan, Candace K.; Ruffo, Riccardo; Hong, Seung Sae; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon nanowires (SiNWs) have the potential to perform as anodes for lithium-ion batteries with a much higher energy d. than graphite.  However, there has been little work in understanding the surface chem. of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on silicon due to the redn. of the electrolyte.  Given that a good, passivating SEI layer plays such a crucial role in graphite anodes, we have characterized the surface compn. and morphol. of the SEI formed on the SiNWs using XPS and SEM.  We have found that the SEI is composed of redn. products similar to that found on graphite electrodes, with Li2CO3 as an important component.  Combined with electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, the results were used to det. the optimal cycling parameters for good cycling.  The role of the native SiO2 as well as the effect of the surface area of the SiNWs on reactivity with the electrolyte were also addressed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoq3JEpW0XP2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPEu1PQqbtAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXjsVChs7g%253D&amp;md5=918b18a7bef2105f9d8c0639e759727a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1149/2.0201514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Clément, R.                         cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Manganese-Based P2-Type Transition Metal Oxides as Sodium-Ion Battery Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Clement, Raphaele J.; Bruce, Peter G.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium transition metal oxides (NaMO2) with a P2 structure exhibit good Na+ ion cond. and are promising sodium-ion battery cathode materials.  Manganese-based compds. have a high working potential vs. Na+/Na, and high capacity.  Yet, the layered nature of these materials means that they are prone to structural rearrangements at high voltage/low Na contents, the phase transformations and Na+ ion/vacancy ordering transitions resulting in capacity fade and poor reversibility.  This review discusses the latest advances in the field and focuses mainly on recent work on NayMn1-xMxO2 (x, y ≤ 1, M = Ni, Mg, Li) compds.  We compare the different lithium and sodium transition metal layered oxides (P2, O3, etc.) and describe the structures and mechanisms obsd. on alkali (de)intercalation.  The strategies used to enhance the electrochem. properties and stabilize the structural framework of sodium transition metal oxides are reviewed.  We show how X-ray diffraction and 7Li/23Na solid-state NMR can be combined to provide a detailed insight into the structural and electronic processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling of these materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3iXys1AM5PLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPEu1PQqbtAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslelu7rE&amp;md5=b77c52de27974cd2c817c5b39a68cd8a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit62" data-doi="10.1149/1.1376116"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.                         cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Can all the lithium be removed from T2-Li2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Zhonghua; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper, it is demonstrated that all the lithium cannot be removed electrochem. from Li2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 at room temp. below 5.4 V vs. Li/Li+.  On the contrary, all the Na atoms can be extd. and reinserted reversibly from P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 electrochem. to form Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 with 0 &lt; x &lt; 0.67.  Both of these results have been confirmed by X-ray diffraction measurements.  Furthermore, when all the Na is removed, [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 adopts the O2 structure with random stacking faults.  The diffraction pattern can be well matched by simulations of these stacking faults using the DIFFaX program.  Lithium can be inserted into [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, however, only about one-half of the lithium which is inserted can be removed.  X-ray diffraction shows that when the Li is inserted into [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, the structure transforms from O2 with stacking faults to O2 without stacking faults.  We speculate upon the reasons for the difference between the behavior of Li and Na in this host system.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrutrXKZiKqqLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPEu1PQqbtAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXlt1KksLc%253D&amp;md5=888c64f1bcb576a2983b22c728d0e90d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit63" data-doi="10.1021/nl502192p"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guan, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, H.                         cas_cit63R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">63</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Stable and Reversible Lithium Storage in SnO2 Nanowires Surface Coated with a Uniform Hollow Shell by Atomic Layer Deposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guan, Cao; Wang, Xinghui; Zhang, Qing; Fan, Zhanxi; Zhang, Hua; Fan, Hong Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 nanowires directly grown on flexible substrates can be a good electrode for a Li ion battery.  However, Sn-based (metal Sn or SnO2) anode materials always suffer from poor stability due to a large vol. expansion during cycling.  The authors use at. layer deposition (ALD) to surface engineer SnO2 nanowires, resulting in a new type of hollowed SnO2-in-TiO2 wire-in-tube nanostructure.  This structure has radically improved rate capability and cycling stability compared to both bare SnO2 nanowires and solid SnO2@TiO2 core-shell nanowire electrodes.  Typically a relatively stable capacity of 393.3 mA-h g-1 was achieved after 1000 charge-discharge cycles at a c.d. of 400 mA g-1, and 241.2 mA-h g-1 at 3200 mA g-1.  It is believed that the uniform hollow TiO2 shell provides stable surface protection and the appropriate-sized gap effectively accommodates the expansion of the interior SnO2 nanowire.  This ALD-enabled method should be general to many other battery anode and cathode materials, providing a new and highly reproducible and controllable technique for improving battery performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpBY3-whWsVZLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPEu1PQqbtAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXht1SqsL%252FK&amp;md5=1050b1f6aa29a862f43127979937652b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref64"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">64</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit64" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201102568"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Alumina-Coated Patterned Amorphous Silicon as the Anode for a Lithium-Ion Battery with High Coulombic Efficiency</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. The electrodes provided stable cycling performance and no cracks were obsd. after the first charge to 2 V.  An alumina-coated electrode had better cycling performance than an uncoated electrode.  The interactions between the anode and electrolyte and coulombic efficiency of each cycle confirm that the alumina acts as a protective layer and prevents side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte.  The fracture resistance of silicon is enhanced by ALD.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3-m2GfsFYQ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6kQI5gGV_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1Wrs7%252FE&amp;md5=7121ca2f54aec3e2a5e3e9414da815ae</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit65" data-doi="10.1149/1.2789299"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Verdier, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">El Ouatani, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dedryvére, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bonhomme, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Biensan, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gonbeau, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">XPS Study on Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and AlPO<sub>4</sub> Coated LiCoO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material for High-Capacity Li Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Intercalation and grafting of benzene derivatives into zinc-aluminum and copper-chromium layered double hydroxide hosts: an XPS monitoring study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fleutot, Solenne; Dupin, Jean-Charles; Renaudin, G.; Martinez, Herve</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">39</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17564-17578</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9076</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report an original strategy to describe, via XPS measurements, the interactions between the org. and the mineral sub-systems within a multifunctional hybrid material.  A tunable layered double hydroxide (LDH) host system, either a Zn2Al- or Cu2Cr-hydrotalcite like compd., is modified with the insertion of the org. guest entities, 4-phenol-sulfonate (HBS) or -carboxylate (HBC).  The resulting interactions were studied at two levels: after the org. mols.' insertion in the host LDH (ionic exchange between the LDH counterions and the org. anions) and after the condensation (grafting) of the org. species onto the mineral layers when thermally treated.  For the inserted material, the main XPS results show a stabilization of the org. mols. within the mineral sheets via H bonding as found elsewhere with FTIR study, the mineral matrix being unchanged.  The XPS signal of the org. mols. slightly changes with a widening of core peaks, attesting to some local surrounding modifications.  When heating up the Zn2Al hybrid material, stronger interactions between org. and inorg. systems appeared from around 80° with some obvious electronic changes as monitored with the XPS S2p signal of the HBS guest mols.  At the same time, the PXRD pattern clearly shows a decrease of the basal spacing according to a two step contraction process which could be interpreted as a progressive org. mol. condensation onto the inorg. layers via iono-covalent bonds.  A Cu-Cr LDH is also studied to probe the same kind of interactions with the HBS mols.  The ability of distortion of such mineral material involves a peculiar process of contraction from 40° with the immediate and effective anchorage of org. mols.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLuIHKnzUWkrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6kQI5gGV_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1eqtbfK&amp;md5=8d2adcb1d12b165593441d8693c6f36e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit67" data-doi="10.1149/2.0331614jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mansour, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kwabi, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Quinlan, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.-C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shao-Horn, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Probing the Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Cycled LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>4</sub> by XPS Using Mg and Synchrotron X-Rays</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Cycled LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 by XPS Using Mg and Synchrotron X-rays</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mansour, Azzam N.; Kwabi, David G.; Quinlan, Ronald A.; Lu, Yi-Chun; Shao-Horn, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">163</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A2911-A2918</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">XPS was used to investigate the surface chem. of high voltage spinel, LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) pos. electrodes cycled 5 and 10 times in Li-cells with 1 M LiPF6 in (3:7) EC:DMC.  The XPS spectra were collected using conventional Mg X-rays with energy of 1253.6 eV as well as synchrotron X-rays with energies of 2493.6 and 3498.4 eV in order to examine the depth distribution of various surface chem. species induced during cycling.  The XPS spectra revealed a 5 - 10 nm surface layer of org. and LixPFyOz-type species formed as result of electrolyte decompn., and a comparatively thinner layer composed of transition metal fluorides and LiF.  These results suggest that electrolyte decompn. is a major contributor to parasitic reactions in LNMO battery electrochem.  Limiting electrolyte decompn. with the use of solvents with wide electrochem. stability windows thus comprises a promising strategy for ensuring the practical feasibility of high voltage spinel materials in future Li-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGptsWUDtd65zrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6kQI5gGV_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvFyntLrI&amp;md5=c13e1868ec5891ab14e675823a2dfbea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit68" data-doi="10.1021/am504701s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Verde, M.                         cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemical composition and morphology of the elevated temperature SEI on graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Andersson, A. M.; Edstrom, K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A1100-A1109</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The relationship between the elevated temp. performance of Li/graphite half-cells and the compn. and morphol. of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on the graphite surface has been investigated for two electrolyte systems: 1 M LiPF6 in ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate EC/DMC (2:1) and 1 M LiBF4 in EC/DMC (2:1).  Precycled cells were stored at different temps. up to 80°C, and the graphite electrodes were analyzed chem. (by XPS) and electrochem. under continued cycling.  Loss of change (for both salts) and of intercalation capacity (for LiBF4) occurred after elevated temp. storage.  The charge loss could be coupled to disappearance of the R-OCO2Li phase from the surface, with subsequent exposure of the graphite surface.  The amt. of LiF increased with increased storage temp., but the LiF morphol. differed between the two electrolyte systems.  A model for the morphol. changes of the SEI layer on storage at elevated temp. is proposed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp42fwlYYVWOLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6kQI5gGV_zQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXnsl2gu7s%253D&amp;md5=aa40d24c5f4f093a15a36de9e286428f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref71"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref71'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">71</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit71" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpclett.5b01727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gauthier, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carney, T. F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Paschos, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bauer, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maglia, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lupart, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lamp, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shao-Horn, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode–Electrolyte Interface in Li-Ion Batteries: Current Understanding and New Insights</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Phys. Chem. Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4653</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4672</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.jpclett.5b01727</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D4653-4672%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BLett.%26author%3DM.%2BGauthier%26author%3DT.%2BJ.%2BCarney%26author%3DA.%2BGrimaud%26author%3DL.%2BGiordano%26author%3DN.%2BPour%26author%3DH.-H.%2BChang%26author%3DD.%2BP.%2BFenning%26author%3DS.%2BF.%2BLux%26author%3DO.%2BPaschos%26author%3DC.%2BBauer%26author%3DF.%2BMaglia%26author%3DS.%2BLupart%26author%3DP.%2BLamp%26author%3DY.%2BShao-Horn%26title%3DElectrode%25E2%2580%2593Electrolyte%2BInterface%2Bin%2BLi-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BCurrent%2BUnderstanding%2Band%2BNew%2BInsights%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpclett.5b01727&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b05326&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpclett.5b01727&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit71R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">71</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Li-Ion Batteries: Current Understanding and New Insights</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gauthier, Magali; Carney, Thomas J.; Grimaud, Alexis; Giordano, Livia; Pour, Nir; Chang, Hao-Hsun; Fenning, David P.; Lux, Simon F.; Paschos, Odysseas; Bauer, Christoph; Maglia, Filippo; Lupart, Saskia; Lamp, Peter; Shao-Horn, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4653-4672</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCLCD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1948-7185</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Understanding reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface (EEI) is essential to developing strategies to enhance cycle life and safety of lithium batteries.  Despite research in the past four decades, there is still limited understanding by what means different components are formed at the EEI and how they affect EEI layer properties.  Findings used to establish the well-known mosaic structure model are reviewed for the EEI (often referred to as solid electrolyte interphase or SEI) on neg. electrodes including lithium, graphite, tin, and silicon.  Much less understanding exists for EEI layers for pos. electrodes.  High-capacity Li-rich layered oxides yLi2-xMnO3·(1-y)Li1-xMO2, which can generate highly reactive species toward the electrolyte via oxygen anion redox, highlight the crit. need to understand reactions with the electrolyte and EEI layers for advanced pos. electrodes.  Recent advances in in situ characterization of well-defined electrode surfaces can provide mechanistic insights and strategies to tailor EEI layer compn. and properties.</div></div><div     </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-ion rechargeable battery with LiCoO2 cathode and non-graphitizable C anode has high energy d.  By using LiPF6 electrolyte dissolved in propylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate soln., excellent cycle performance was obtained even at a moderately high temp., because (1) LiCoO2 remained stable, and (2) non-graphitizable C exhibited a good cyclability with respect to Li-doping/undoping capability.  Although a thin film is formed on the C surface during charge and discharge cycling, the discharge capacity degrdn. is only 10-20% after 500 cycles.  Furthermore, even if the cell is overcharged, safety can be attained by (1) providing an anti-overcharging safety device which operates when Li2CO3 in the cathode is decompd. and (2) using a polyolefin separator which shuts down at a high temp. due to overcharge current.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolNFbZJp4t1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljURpqFSaNiAA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2cXlvVenurY%253D&amp;md5=9410cf64e0551589d7385a92408e1585</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/35104644"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Armand, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Issues and Challenges Facing Rechargeable Lithium Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nature</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2001</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">414</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">359</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">367</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Ongoing research strategies are highlighted, and the challenges that remain regarding the synthesis, characterization, electrochem. performance, and safety of these systems are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGowMxv9DmkLwrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljURpqFSaNiAA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXovFGitrY%253D&amp;md5=944485672a9bdf09f6e6a7a199bf3d43</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seo, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin sulfide-reduced graphene oxide (SnS2-rGO) composite material is investigated as an advanced anode material for Na-ion batteries.  It can deliver a reversible capacity of 630 mAh g-1 with negligible capacity loss and exhibits superb rate performance.  Here, the energy storage mechanism of this SnS2-rGO anode and the crit. mechanistic role of rGO will be revealed in detail.  A synergistic mechanism involving conversion and alloying reactions is proposed based on our synchrotron X-ray diffraction (SXRD) and in situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) results.  Contrary to what has been proposed in the literature, we detd. that Na2S2 forms instead of Na2S at the fully discharge state.  The as-formed Na2S2 works as a matrix to relieve the strain from the huge vol. expansion of the Na-Sn alloy reaction, shown in the high resoln. transmission electron microscope (HRTEM).  In addn., the Raman spectra results suggest that the rGO not only assists the material to have better electrochem. performance by preventing particle agglomeration of the active material but also coordinates with Na-ions through electrostatic interaction during the first cycle.  The unique reaction mechanism in SnS2-rGO offers a well-balanced approach for sodium storage to deliver high capacity, long-cycle life, and superior rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoQ0HQeDv131bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh_5MRwlC4u6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Sgs7vN&amp;md5=12f7ae16a53d72721aecf9a2bd718688</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles. However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRG1Bub04s8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYP7MBTYVQBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlCjur7N&amp;md5=6d0a96f9e9421c02a9f3b48744267752</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1142/S1793604713300016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (x, y, z ≤ 1) cathode materials are investigated using 23Na and 7Li solid-state NMR (ssNMR).  The interpretation of the complex paramagnetic NMR data obtained for various electrochem.-cycled NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 and NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 samples is assisted by state-of-the-art hybrid Hartree-Fock/d. functional theory calcns. This is the first time that exptl. and theor. evidence are provided for fast Na-ion motion (on the timescale of the NMR expts.) in the interlayer space in P2-type NaxTMO2 materials.  A full assignment of the 7Li NMR data confirms that Li substitution delays the P2 to O2 phase transformation taking place in NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 over the range 1/3 ≥ xNa ≥ 0. 23Na ssNMR data demonstrate that NaxNi1/3Mn2/3O2 samples charged to ≥3.7 V are extremely moisture sensitive once they are removed from the cell, water mols. being readily intercalated within the P2 layers leading to an addnl. Na signal between 400 and 250 ppm.  By contrast, the lithiated material NaxLi0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66O2 shows no sign of hydration until it is charged to ≥4.4 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium transition metal oxides (NaMO2) with a P2 structure exhibit good Na+ ion cond. and are promising sodium-ion battery cathode materials.  Manganese-based compds. have a high working potential vs. Na+/Na, and high capacity.  Yet, the layered nature of these materials means that they are prone to structural rearrangements at high voltage/low Na contents, the phase transformations and Na+ ion/vacancy ordering transitions resulting in capacity fade and poor reversibility.  This review discusses the latest advances in the field and focuses mainly on recent work on NayMn1-xMxO2 (x, y ≤ 1, M = Ni, Mg, Li) compds.  We compare the different lithium and sodium transition metal layered oxides (P2, O3, etc.) and describe the structures and mechanisms obsd. on alkali (de)intercalation.  The strategies used to enhance the electrochem. properties and stabilize the structural framework of sodium transition metal oxides are reviewed.  We show how X-ray diffraction and 7Li/23Na solid-state NMR can be combined to provide a detailed insight into the structural and electronic processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling of these materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3iXys1AM5PLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lix_wkrlREmZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslelu7rE&amp;md5=b77c52de27974cd2c817c5b39a68cd8a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1021/cm403855t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-substituted layered P2-Na0.80[Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66]O2 is studied as an advanced cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRaU2CEikYCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lix_wkrlREmZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvVSluw%253D%253D&amp;md5=aebb4423baa75fe925ec0b0c2f7c0955</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.12.083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhong, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">McDonald, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07, 0.14) are prepd. using a conventional solid state method and for the first time developed as promising cathode materials for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.  The x-ray diffraction patterns show that Zn2+ ions are successfully incorporated into the lattice of the Na-Ni-Mn-O system and the P2-type structure remains unchanged after substitution.  The introduction of Zn2+ in the Na-Ni-Mn-O system can effectively overcome the drawback of voltage decay when charged to a higher cutoff voltage (&gt;4.0 V), and significantly improve capacity retention compared to the unsubstituted material during cycling.  In addn., a smoother charge/discharge profile can be obsd. between 3.0 and 4.0 V for Zn-substituted samples, demonstrating that Na+/vacancy ordering can be suppressed during sodium insertion/extn.  Na0.66Ni0.26Zn0.07Mn0.67O2 can deliver an initial capacity of 132 mA-h/g at 12 mA/g with a high av. voltage of 3.6 V and a capacity retention of 89% after 30 cycles.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy measurements demonstrate that Zn substitution is an effective way to limit the increase of inter-particle contact resistance by suppressing any possible irreversible phase transformation found at low sodium contents.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrmbpOEPj4Ld7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lix_wkrlREmZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFOqs7fN&amp;md5=0a2dab09a85de3fe2f0bd70e6b9ec3a0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06701"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, G.-L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhong, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gong, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">McDonald, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type sodium nickel manganese oxide-based cathode materials with higher energy densities are prime candidates for applications in rechargeable sodium ion batteries.  A systematic study combining in situ high energy X-ray diffraction (HEXRD), ex situ X-ray absorption fine spectroscopy (XAFS), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and solid-state NMR (SS-NMR) techniques was carried out to gain a deep insight into the structural evolution of P2-Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07) during cycling.  In situ HEXRD and ex situ TEM measurements indicate that an irreversible phase transition occurs upon sodium insertion-extn. of Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.67O2.  Zinc doping of this system results in a high structural reversibility.  XAFS measurements indicate that both materials are almost completely dependent on the Ni4+/Ni3+/Ni2+ redox couple to provide charge/discharge capacity.  SS-NMR measurements indicate that both reversible and irreversible migration of transition metal ions into the sodium layer occurs in the material at the fully charged state.  The irreversible migration of transition metal ions triggers a structural distortion, leading to the obsd. capacity and voltage fading.  Our results allow a new understanding of the importance of improving the stability of transition metal layers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqyUPPhcNq0kLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lix_wkrlREmZw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht12jsbvM&amp;md5=f3a446894cd9f89f799beef61f2b49ee</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201602202"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, P.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">You, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, Y.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, L.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.-G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Suppressing the P2–O2 Phase Transition of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by Magnesium Substitution for Improved Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew. An at. layer deposition (ALD) is a representative thin film technol. with exceptional capabilities in developing at.-precisely conformal films.  It has been demonstrated to be a promise strategy of solving emerging issues in advanced elec. energy storage (EES) devices via the surface modification and/or the fabrication of complex nanostructured materials.  In this review, the recent developments and significances on how ALD improves the performance of Li-S batteries were discussed in detail.  Significant attention mainly focused on the various strategies with the use of ALD to refine the electrochem. interfaces and cell configurations.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We demonstrate that silicon nanowire Li-ion battery anodes that are conformally coated with TiO2 using at. layer deposition (ALD) show a remarkable performance improvement.  The coulombic efficiency is increased to ∼99%, among the highest ever reported for silicon nanowires, as compared to 95% for the baseline uncoated samples.  The capacity retention after 100 cycles for the nanocomposite is twice as high as that of the baseline at 0.1C (60% vs. 30%), and more than three times higher at 5C (34% vs. 10%).  We also demonstrate that the microstructure of the coatings is critically important for achieving this effect.  Titanium dioxide coatings with an as-deposited anatase structure are nowhere near as effective as amorphous ones, the latter proving much more resistant to delamination from the silicon nanowire core.  We use TEM to demonstrate that upon lithiation the amorphous coating develops a highly dispersed nanostructure comprised of cryst. LiTiO2 and a secondary amorphous phase.  Electron energy loss spectroscopy combined with bulk and surface anal. techniques are employed to highlight the passivating effect of TiO2, which results in significantly fewer cycling-induced electrolyte decompn. products as compared to the bare nanowires.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpC8kF3vsCy8bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTlPBKksiKaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOgtrbK&amp;md5=3e4312fca71381ff3178f9ac491fa1a8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1149/1.3258274"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jung, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin at. layer deposition coatings enhance the performance of lithium-ion batteries.  Previous studies have demonstrated that LiCoO2 cathode powders coated with metal oxides with thicknesses of ∼100 to 1000 Å grown using wet chem. techniques improved lithium-ion battery performance.  In this study, LiCoO2 powders were coated with conformal Al2O3 at. layer deposition films with thicknesses of only ∼3 to 4 Å established using two at. layer deposition cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This study reports the prepn. of Al2O3 and TiO2 coatings on the as-prepd. LiCoO2 electrodes using at. layer deposition.  A thin Al2O3 at. layer deposition coating was shown to eliminate capacity fading effectively during repeated charging and discharging, whereas a TiO2 coating led to significant improvement only at high cycle nos.  An anal. of the differential capacity vs. potential curves suggests that this poorer cycling performance could be related to the participation of the TiO2 thin film in the redox reaction.  Graphical representation of the energy levels of the various at. layer deposition coatings on LiCoO2 during charging and discharging indicated that the redox current is impeded at the Al2O3-LiCoO2 junction, whereas electrons and holes were energetic enough to flow into the TiO2 because of the smaller band gap energy.  The barrier between the valence band maxima of TiO2 and LiCoO2 expands as the charge-discharge cycle no. increases, eventually making TiO2 redox-inactive.  These conclusions are supported by both XPS spectra and the cycle performance in the established literature refs.  Our results suggest that large band gap materials should be considered to be potentially useful at. layer deposition coatings on cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrJ1rShZf5YALVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhTlPBKksiKaQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xkt1Omt78%253D&amp;md5=53c395a4b705634739068a74f348d1a7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1007/s00339-010-6097-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Atomic layer deposition was used to deposit ZrO2 directly on Li4Ti5O12 electrode to improve its electrochem. performance.  The thickness of the deposited ZrO2 was controlled by adjusting at. layer deposition cycles from 0 to 1, 2, 5, 10 and 50.  The Li4Ti5O12 electrodes with and without ZrO2 coating were characterized by SEM, energy dispersive x-ray spectroscopy, high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic charge-discharge tests.  The cyclic voltammetry results indicated that ZrO2 coating with 2, 5 and 10 at. layer deposition cycles could effectively reduce the electrochem. polarization of the Li4Ti5O12 electrode.  Charge-discharge test revealed that the Li4Ti5O12 electrodes with 1-, 2- and 5-cycle ZrO2 coating exhibited higher specific capacity, better cycling performance and rate capability than the pristine Li4Ti5O12 in a voltage range of 0.1-2.5 V.  However, ZrO2 coating with more than 5 at. layer deposition cycles could lead to degraded performance of Li4Ti5O12.  Mechanism for the enhanced electrochem. performance of Li4Ti5O12 was explored by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and the reason was attributed to the suppressed formation of solid electrolyte interphase and the improved electron transport by ultrathin ZrO2 coating.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYJtEc1f55O7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy5kIq2BBb4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXjvFegsLY%253D&amp;md5=4f3e7e1997a6aec3f1aa9fbbfe1ffa88</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2005.11.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cathode materials modified by surface coating for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, C.; Zhang, H. P.; Fu, L. J.; Liu, H.; Wu, Y. P.; Rahm, E.; Holze, R.; Wu, H. Q.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2006</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">51</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3872-3883</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
