            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recent research results confirm the importance of structural surface features of cathode materials for their electrochem. performance.  Modification by coating is an important method to achieve improved electrochem. performance, and the latest progress is summarized.  When the surface of cathode materials including LiCoO2, LiNiO2, LiMn2O4 and LiMnO2 is coated with oxides such as MgO, Al2O3, SiO2, TiO2, ZnO, SnO2, ZrO2, Li2O·2B2O3-glass and other materials, the coatings prevent the direct contact with the electrolyte soln., suppress phase transition, improve the structural stability, and decrease the disorder of cations in crystal sites.  As a result, side reactions and heat generation during cycling are decreased.  Accompanying actions, such as suppression of Mn2+ dissoln., increase in cond. and removal of HF from electrolyte solns., have been obsd.  Consequently, marked improvement of electrochem. performance of electrode materials including reversible capacity, Coulomb efficiency in the first cycle, cycling behavior, rate capability and overcharge tolerance has been achieved.  Further directions are suggested for the surface modification of electrode materials.  With further understanding of the effects of the surface structure of cathode materials on lithium intercalation and de-intercalation, better and/or cheaper cathode materials from surface modification will come up in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpsZVjB0yoj1bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy5kIq2BBb4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XktleisLc%253D&amp;md5=6489647bf7fcfc3bcdc4dd3638cab5f7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1039/C3EE42704H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banis, M.                         rightTab-cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Atomic layer deposition of solid-state electrolyte coated cathode materials with superior high-voltage cycling behavior for lithium ion battery application</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xifei; Liu, Jian; Banis, Mohammad Norouzi; Lushington, Andrew; Li, Ruying; Cai, Mei; Sun, Xueliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 (NMC) is a highly promising cathode material for use in lithium ion batteries; unfortunately, its poor cycling performance at high cutoff voltages hinders its commercialization.  In this study, for the first time, we employ at. layer deposition (ALD) to coat lithium tantalum oxide, a solid-state electrolyte, with varying thicknesses on NMC in an attempt to improve battery performance.  Our results indicate that utilization of a solid-state electrolyte as a coating material for NMC significantly improves performance at high cutoff voltages but is strongly dependent on coating thicknesses.  Our investigation revealed that a thicker coating proved to be beneficial in preventing cathode material dissoln. into the electrolyte and aided in maintaining the microstructure of NMC.  Consequently, a thicker ALD coating resulted in increased electrochem. impedance of the cathode.  The results of this study indicate that an optimized coating thickness is needed in order to strike a balance between maintaining structural stability while minimizing electrochem. impedance.  The coating thicknesses are functionally specific, and for the best improvement of a cathode, a particular coating thickness should be sought.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr432_eespCI7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy5kIq2BBb4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFaksLc%253D&amp;md5=a4fb112025ec7d2324d0a78011c39ae9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.08.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meng, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banis, M.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiCoO2 in the com. lithium ion batteries has been suffering from its poor cycling performance at high cutoff voltages.  In this study, the authors employ an at. layer deposition (ALD) technique to surface-modify a LiCoO2 material with various thickness-controlled metal oxide (TiO2, ZrO2 and Al2O3) coatings to improve its battery performance.  The effects of the metal oxide coatings on the electrochem. performance of LiCoO2 electrode are studied in detail.  It is demonstrated that a uniform and dense coating via the ALD route on LiCoO2 powder can lower the battery performance due to an obvious decrease in lithium diffusion and electron transport with the coating layers.  In contrast, it is revealed that a direct coating on prefabricated LiCoO2 electrodes performs much better than a coating on LiCoO2 powders.  It is further disclosed that the improved electrochem. performance of coated LiCoO2 electrode is highly dependent on the coating materials.  Of the three coating materials, the Al2O3 coating results in the best cycling stability while the ZrO2 coating contributes to the best rate capability.  It is thus suggested that the coating materials are functionally specific, and for the best improvement of a cathode, a particular coating material should be sought.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQqKKnGMru9LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy5kIq2BBb4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhs12lsbjN&amp;md5=dd244c78955b2b23a363be3adf54e652</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200166"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Armand, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Disodium Terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) as High Performance Anode Material for Low-Cost Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The P2 type Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 is a high-voltage cathode material for Na-ion batteries with a theor. capacity of 173 mA h/g and a long operation voltage plateau of 4.2 V.  However, the material has exhibited unstable cycling performance within the high-voltage window, which severely limits its application.  Moreover, its capacity decay mechanism is still unclear.  In this study, we first investigate the difference between as-prepd. and after-cycling Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 samples, and then confirmed that the transition metal oxide layer exfoliation assocd. with the crystal phase transition during Na ion extn. and insertion is the main cause of capacity fading.  The Al2O3 coated Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 with enhanced cycling performance was prepd. by taking the benefit of Al2O3 coating.  The Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 sample without any surface modification presented a 164 mA h/g initial specific discharge capacity within the voltage window from 2.5 V to 4.3 V, and the capacity decayed to 44 mA h/g at the 300th cycle, resulting in only a 26.8% retention.  In contrast, the Al2O3-coated Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 presented a similar initial capacity, but with an enhanced 73.2% retention after 300 cycles.  The enhanced cycling stability obsd. in after-cycling characterization and anal. confirms that the Al2O3 surface coating can effectively suppress the unfavorable side reaction at high voltage and the exfoliation of the metal oxide layers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqwO8gmvj_Iv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy5kIq2BBb4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFCrsbzM&amp;md5=65d57c83995b13acf248dff048736de2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b02158"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sina, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Parikh, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Uekert, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shahbazian, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Devaraj, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meng, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid org.-inorg. materials for high-efficiency, low-cost photovoltaic devices have seen rapid progress since the introduction of lead based perovskites and solid-state hole transport layers.  Although majority of the materials used for perovskite solar cells (PSC) are introduced from dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs), the presence of a perovskite capping layer as opposed to a single dye mol. (in DSSCs) changes the interactions between the various layers in perovskite solar cells. 4-tert-Butylpyridine (tBP), commonly used in PSCs, is assumed to function as a charge recombination inhibitor, similar to DSSCs.  However, the presence of a perovskite capping layer calls for a re-evaluation of its function in PSCs.  Using TEM (transmission electron microscopy), we first confirm the role of tBP as a HTL morphol. controller in PSCs.  Our observations suggest that tBP significantly improves the uniformity of the HTL and avoids accumulation of Li salt.  We also study degrdn. pathways by using FTIR (Fourier transform IR spectroscopy) and APT (atom probe tomog.) to investigate and visualize in 3-dimensions the moisture content assocd. with the Li salt.  Long-term effects, over 1000 h, due to evapn. of tBP have also been studied.  Based on our findings, a PSC failure mechanism assocd. with the morphol. change of the HTL is proposed. tBP, the morphol. controller in HTL, plays a key role in this process, and thus this study highlights the need for additive materials with higher b.ps. for consistent long-term performance of PSCs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpKVK_Iqh022bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljRqFrSmPZ7DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtlOqu7vO&amp;md5=2ecb5f8cb1dc4bea8bc1871415bbd58f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1016/j.ultramic.2010.11.027"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Focused ion beam (FIB) instruments have proven to be an invaluable tool for transmission electron microscopy (TEM) sample prepn.  FIBs enable relatively easy and site-specific cross-sectioning of different classes of materials.  However, damage mechanisms due to ion bombardment and possible beam heating effects in materials limit the usefulness of FIBs.  Materials with adequate heat cond. do not suffer from beam heating during FIB prepn., and artifacts in materials such as metals and ceramics are primarily limited to defect generation and Ga implantation.  However, in materials such as polymers or biol. structures, where heat cond. is low, beam heating can also be a problem.  In order to examine FIB damage in polymers we have undertaken a systematic study by exposing sections of a PS-b-PMMA block copolymer to the ion beam at varying beam currents and sample temps.  The sections were then examd. by TEM and SEM and analyzed using electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS).  Our empirical results show beam heating in polymers due to FIB prepn. can be limited by maintaining a low beam current (≤100 pA) during milling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoyrV1CjnoIMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljRqFrSmPZ7DA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXitFWqtbo%253D&amp;md5=4e385c1960825439e67d64b76c09f784</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2013.03.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Malmgren, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ciosek, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hahlin, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gustafsson, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gorgoi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rensmo, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Edström, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Comparing Anode and Cathode Electrode/electrolyte Interface Composition and Morphology Using Soft and Hard X-Ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Fine-tuning of particle size and morphol. has been shown to result in differential material performance in the area of secondary lithium-ion batteries.  For instance, redn. of particle size to the nanoregime typically leads to better transport of electrochem. active species by increasing the amt. of reaction sites as a result of higher electrode surface area.  The spinel-phase oxide LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO), was prepd. using a sol-gel based template synthesis to yield nanowire morphol. without any addnl. binders or electronic conducting agents.  Therefore, proper experimentation of the nanosize effect can be achieved in this study.  The spinel phase LMNO is a high energy electrode material currently being explored for use in lithium-ion batteries, with a specific capacity of 146 mAh/g and high-voltage plateau at ∼4.7 V (vs. Li/Li+).  However, research has shown that extensive electrolyte decompn. and the formation of a surface passivation layer results when LMNO is implemented as a cathode in electrochem. cells.  As a result of the high surface area assocd. with nanosized particles, manganese ion dissoln. results in capacity fading over prolonged cycling.  In order to prevent these detrimental effects without compromising electrochem. performance, various coating methods were explored.  In this work, TiO2 and Al2O3 thin films were deposited using at. layer deposition (ALD) on the surface of LNMO particles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of depositing ultrathin (&lt;1 nm) Al2O3 coatings on LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) particles using at. layer deposition (ALD) are presented.  Promising electrochem. performance of the Al2O3 ALD coated LNMO at 30° is demonstrated in not only significantly improved coulombic efficiency, cycle retention, and rate capability, but also in dramatically suppressed self-discharge and dissoln. of transition metals.  Combined analyses by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, ex-situ XPS, and ex-situ time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry reveal that the solid electrolyte interphase layer on the Al2O3 ALD coated LNMO is much thinner and contains fewer org. species than the one on the bare LNMO. Also, fluorination of Al2O3 ALD layer upon repeated charge-discharge cycling is confirmed, and this can account for the capacity increases during the initial charge-discharge cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin conformal coatings of the Li ion conductor, Li Al oxide (LiAlO2), were evaluated for their ability to improve the electrochem. stability of LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/graphite Li-ion batteries.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy confirmed the ion conducting character of the LiAlO2 films.  Complementary simulations of the activation barriers in these layers match exptl. results very well.  LiAlO2 films were subsequently sep. deposited onto LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 and graphite electrodes.  Increased electrochem. stability was obsd., esp. in the full cells, which was attributed to the role of the coatings as phys. barriers against side reactions at the electrode-electrolyte interface.  By comparing data from full cells where the coatings were applied to either electrode, the dominating failure mechanism is the diffusion of transition metal ions from the cathode to the anode.  The LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/graphite full cell with &lt;1 nm LiAlO2 on the pos. electrode exhibited a discharge capacity of 92 mA-h g-1 at C/3 rate.  The chem. underpinnings of stable performance were revealed by soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The 5 V spinel cathode LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 with cation disorder in the 16d octahedral sites has been surface modified with 2 wt.% nanosize Al2O3, ZnO, Bi2O3, and AlPO4 by an electrostatic self-assembly method.  The bare and surface-modified samples have been characterized by x-ray diffraction, FTIR spectroscopy, high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, charge-discharge measurements in lithium batteries, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and XPS.  The surface-modified samples exhibit better cycling performance, better rate capability, and better rate capability retention during cycling compared to the bare sample.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy and XPS studies show that the inferior electrochem. performances of the bare LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 are closely related to the formation of thick solid-electrolyte interfacial (SEI) layer at the high operating voltages of ∼5 V.  Surface modifications with nanosize Al2O3, ZnO, Bi2O3, and AlPO4 suppress the formation of thick SEI layers on LiMn1.42Ni0.42Co0.16O4 and thereby improve the electrochem. performances significantly.  Moreover, the differences in the surface compns. formed during the annealing or electrochem. cycling processes also influence the electrochem. properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVoi9qtM6_n7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj2JV8ejAjDMQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXjslWmtb4%253D&amp;md5=1bf7a163740043e6ed54a681306e1155</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2010.11.124"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Riley, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Combustion synthesized Li(Ni1/3Mn1/3Co1/3)O2 particles are coated with thin, conformal layers of Al2O3 by at. layer deposition (ALD).                         rightTab-cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Diffusion in Al2O3: A Distinct Perspective Compared with Lithium Ion Diffusion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jung, Sung Chul; Kim, Hyung-Jin; Choi, Jang Wook; Han, Young-Kyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Surface coating of active materials has been one of the most effective strategies to mitigate undesirable side reactions and thereby improve the overall battery performance.  In this direction, aluminum oxide (Al2O3) is one of the most widely adopted coating materials due to its easy synthesis and low material cost.  Nevertheless, the effect of Al2O3 coating on carrier ion diffusion has been investigated mainly for Li ion batteries, and the corresponding understanding for emerging Na ion batteries is currently missing.  Using ab initio mol. dynamics calcns., herein, we first find that, unlike lithiation, sodiation of Al2O3 is thermodynamically unfavorable.  Nonetheless, there can still exist a threshold in the Na ion content in Al2O3 before further diffusion into the adjacent active material, delivering a new insight that both thermodn. and kinetics should be taken into account to describe ionic diffusion in any material media.  Furthermore, Na ion diffusivity in NaxAl2O3 turns out to be much higher than Li ion diffusivity in LixAl2O3, a result opposite to the conventional stereotype based on the at. radius consideration.  While hopping between the O-rich trapping sites via an Na-O bond breaking/making process is identified as the main Na ion diffusion mechanism, the weaker Na-O bond strength than the Li-O counterpart turns out to be the origin of the superior diffusivity of Na ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGppsVF5CuqZkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj2JV8ejAjDMQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslSkurzO&amp;md5=f9cd99fb338931129cb20320c9291c80</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1149/2.100202jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ender, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Weber, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ivers-Tiffée, E.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Analysis of Three-Electrode Setups for AC-Impedance Measurements on Lithium-Ion Cells by FEM Simulations</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) has become a std. electrolyte additive for use with Si neg. electrodes, but how FEC affects solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation on the Si anode's surface is still not well understood.  Herein, SEI formed from LiPF6-based carbonate electrolytes, with and without FEC, were studied on 50 nm thick amorphous Si thin film electrodes to understand the role of FEC on Si electrode surface reactions.  In contrast to previous work, anhyd. and anoxic techniques were used to prevent air and moisture contamination of prepd. SEI films.  This allowed for accurate characterization of the SEI structure and compn. by XPS and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry depth profiling.  FEC redn. leads to F- ion and LiF formation, consistent with previous computational and exptl. results.  Surprisingly, also these species decrease Li-ion soly. and increase the reactivity of the Si surface.  The effectiveness of FEC at improving the Coulombic efficiency and capacity retention is due to F- ion formation from redn. of the electrolyte, which leads to the chem. attack of any Si-oxide surface passivation layers and the formation of a kinetically stable SEI comprising predominately LiF and Li oxide.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSv3p3Tp69f7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj2JV8ejAjDMQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1OqsbzJ&amp;md5=e86fa14fac83156dfafd6d6eed502ba8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.01.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon nanowires (SiNWs) have the potential to perform as anodes for lithium-ion batteries with a much higher energy d. than graphite.  However, there has been little work in understanding the surface chem. of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on silicon due to the redn. of the electrolyte.  Given that a good, passivating SEI layer plays such a crucial role in graphite anodes, we have characterized the surface compn. and morphol. of the SEI formed on the SiNWs using XPS and SEM.  We have found that the SEI is composed of redn. products similar to that found on graphite electrodes, with Li2CO3 as an important component.  Combined with electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, the results were used to det. the optimal cycling parameters for good cycling.  The role of the native SiO2 as well as the effect of the surface area of the SiNWs on reactivity with the electrolyte were also addressed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoq3JEpW0XP2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj2JV8ejAjDMQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXjsVChs7g%253D&amp;md5=918b18a7bef2105f9d8c0639e759727a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1149/2.0201514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Clément, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium transition metal oxides (NaMO2) with a P2 structure exhibit good Na+ ion cond. and are promising sodium-ion battery cathode materials.  Manganese-based compds. have a high working potential vs. Na+/Na, and high capacity.  Yet, the layered nature of these materials means that they are prone to structural rearrangements at high voltage/low Na contents, the phase transformations and Na+ ion/vacancy ordering transitions resulting in capacity fade and poor reversibility.  This review discusses the latest advances in the field and focuses mainly on recent work on NayMn1-xMxO2 (x, y ≤ 1, M = Ni, Mg, Li) compds.  We compare the different lithium and sodium transition metal layered oxides (P2, O3, etc.) and describe the structures and mechanisms obsd. on alkali (de)intercalation.  The strategies used to enhance the electrochem. properties and stabilize the structural framework of sodium transition metal oxides are reviewed.  We show how X-ray diffraction and 7Li/23Na solid-state NMR can be combined to provide a detailed insight into the structural and electronic processes occurring upon electrochem. cycling of these materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3iXys1AM5PLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslelu7rE&amp;md5=b77c52de27974cd2c817c5b39a68cd8a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref62" class="rightTabRefNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit62" data-doi="10.1149/1.1376116"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper, it is demonstrated that all the lithium cannot be removed electrochem. from Li2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 at room temp. below 5.4 V vs. Li/Li+.  On the contrary, all the Na atoms can be extd. and reinserted reversibly from P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 electrochem. to form Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 with 0 &lt; x &lt; 0.67.  Both of these results have been confirmed by X-ray diffraction measurements.  Furthermore, when all the Na is removed, [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 adopts the O2 structure with random stacking faults.  The diffraction pattern can be well matched by simulations of these stacking faults using the DIFFaX program.  Lithium can be inserted into [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, however, only about one-half of the lithium which is inserted can be removed.  X-ray diffraction shows that when the Li is inserted into [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, the structure transforms from O2 with stacking faults to O2 without stacking faults.  We speculate upon the reasons for the difference between the behavior of Li and Na in this host system.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrutrXKZiKqqLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXlt1KksLc%253D&amp;md5=888c64f1bcb576a2983b22c728d0e90d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref63" class="rightTabRefNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit63" data-doi="10.1021/nl502192p"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guan, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 nanowires directly grown on flexible substrates can be a good electrode for a Li ion battery.  However, Sn-based (metal Sn or SnO2) anode materials always suffer from poor stability due to a large vol. expansion during cycling.  The authors use at. layer deposition (ALD) to surface engineer SnO2 nanowires, resulting in a new type of hollowed SnO2-in-TiO2 wire-in-tube nanostructure.  This structure has radically improved rate capability and cycling stability compared to both bare SnO2 nanowires and solid SnO2@TiO2 core-shell nanowire electrodes.  Typically a relatively stable capacity of 393.3 mA-h g-1 was achieved after 1000 charge-discharge cycles at a c.d. of 400 mA g-1, and 241.2 mA-h g-1 at 3200 mA g-1.  It is believed that the uniform hollow TiO2 shell provides stable surface protection and the appropriate-sized gap effectively accommodates the expansion of the interior SnO2 nanowire.  This ALD-enabled method should be general to many other battery anode and cathode materials, providing a new and highly reproducible and controllable technique for improving battery performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpBY3-whWsVZLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXht1SqsL%252FK&amp;md5=1050b1f6aa29a862f43127979937652b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref64"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref64" class="rightTabRefNumLink">64</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit64" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201102568"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Alumina-Coated Patterned Amorphous Silicon as the Anode for a Lithium-Ion Battery with High Coulombic Efficiency</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report an original strategy to describe, via XPS measurements, the interactions between the org. and the mineral sub-systems within a multifunctional hybrid material.  A tunable layered double hydroxide (LDH) host system, either a Zn2Al- or Cu2Cr-hydrotalcite like compd., is modified with the insertion of the org. guest entities, 4-phenol-sulfonate (HBS) or -carboxylate (HBC).  The resulting interactions were studied at two levels: after the org. mols.' insertion in the host LDH (ionic exchange between the LDH counterions and the org. anions) and after the condensation (grafting) of the org. species onto the mineral layers when thermally treated.  For the inserted material, the main XPS results show a stabilization of the org. mols. within the mineral sheets via H bonding as found elsewhere with FTIR study, the mineral matrix being unchanged.  The XPS signal of the org. mols. slightly changes with a widening of core peaks, attesting to some local surrounding modifications.  When heating up the Zn2Al hybrid material, stronger interactions between org. and inorg. systems appeared from around 80° with some obvious electronic changes as monitored with the XPS S2p signal of the HBS guest mols.  At the same time, the PXRD pattern clearly shows a decrease of the basal spacing according to a two step contraction process which could be interpreted as a progressive org. mol. condensation onto the inorg. layers via iono-covalent bonds.  A Cu-Cr LDH is also studied to probe the same kind of interactions with the HBS mols.  The ability of distortion of such mineral material involves a peculiar process of contraction from 40° with the immediate and effective anchorage of org. mols.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoLuIHKnzUWkrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1eqtbfK&amp;md5=8d2adcb1d12b165593441d8693c6f36e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref67" class="rightTabRefNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit67" data-doi="10.1149/2.0331614jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mansour, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kwabi, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Quinlan, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.-C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shao-Horn, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Probing the Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Cycled LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>4</sub> by XPS Using Mg and Synchrotron X-Rays</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">163</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">A2911</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">A2918</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/2.0331614jes</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Cycled LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 by XPS Using Mg and Synchrotron X-rays</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mansour, Azzam N.; Kwabi, David G.; Quinlan, Ronald A.; Lu, Yi-Chun; Shao-Horn, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">163</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A2911-A2918</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">XPS was used to investigate the surface chem. of high voltage spinel, LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) pos. electrodes cycled 5 and 10 times in Li-cells with 1 M LiPF6 in (3:7) EC:DMC.  The XPS spectra were collected using conventional Mg X-rays with energy of 1253.6 eV as well as synchrotron X-rays with energies of 2493.6 and 3498.4 eV in order to examine the depth distribution of various surface chem. species induced during cycling.  The XPS spectra revealed a 5 - 10 nm surface layer of org. and LixPFyOz-type species formed as result of electrolyte decompn., and a comparatively thinner layer composed of transition metal fluorides and LiF.  These results suggest that electrolyte decompn. is a major contributor to parasitic reactions in LNMO battery electrochem.  Limiting electrolyte decompn. with the use of solvents with wide electrochem. stability windows thus comprises a promising strategy for ensuring the practical feasibility of high voltage spinel materials in future Li-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGptsWUDtd65zrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvFyntLrI&amp;md5=c13e1868ec5891ab14e675823a2dfbea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref68" class="rightTabRefNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit68" data-doi="10.1021/am504701s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Verde, M. G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carroll, K. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Baggetto, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Veith, G. M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meng, Y. S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of Morphology and Manganese Valence on the Voltage Fade and Capacity Retention of Li[Li<sub>2/12</sub>Ni<sub>3/12</sub> Mn<sub>7/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">18868</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">18877</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/am504701s</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref69" class="rightTabRefNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit69" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2004.03.049"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Edström, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gustafsson, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Thomas, J. O.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Cathode–electrolyte Interface in the Li-Ion Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim. Acta</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2004</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">50</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">397</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">403</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2004.03.049</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref70" class="rightTabRefNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit70" data-doi="10.1149/1.1397771"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Andersson, A. M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Edström, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chemical Composition and Morphology of the Elevated Temperature SEI on Graphite</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2001</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">148</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">A1100</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">A1109</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.1397771</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemical composition and morphology of the elevated temperature SEI on graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Andersson, A. M.; Edstrom, K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A1100-A1109</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The relationship between the elevated temp. performance of Li/graphite half-cells and the compn. and morphol. of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed on the graphite surface has been investigated for two electrolyte systems: 1 M LiPF6 in ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate EC/DMC (2:1) and 1 M LiBF4 in EC/DMC (2:1).  Precycled cells were stored at different temps. up to 80°C, and the graphite electrodes were analyzed chem. (by XPS) and electrochem. under continued cycling.  Loss of change (for both salts) and of intercalation capacity (for LiBF4) occurred after elevated temp. storage.  The charge loss could be coupled to disappearance of the R-OCO2Li phase from the surface, with subsequent exposure of the graphite surface.  The amt. of LiF increased with increased storage temp., but the LiF morphol. differed between the two electrolyte systems.  A model for the morphol. changes of the SEI layer on storage at elevated temp. is proposed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp42fwlYYVWOLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSeIad3YNYAQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXnsl2gu7s%253D&amp;md5=aa40d24c5f4f093a15a36de9e286428f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref71"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref71" class="rightTabRefNumLink">71</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit71" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpclett.5b01727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gauthier, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carney, T. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Grimaud, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Giordano, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pour, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chang, H.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fenning, D. P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lux, S. F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Paschos, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bauer, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maglia, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lupart, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lamp, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shao-Horn, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode–Electrolyte Interface in Li-Ion Batteries: Current Understanding and New Insights</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Phys. Chem. Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4653</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4672</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.jpclett.5b01727</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit71R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">71</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode-Electrolyte Interface in Li-Ion Batteries: Current Understanding and New Insights</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gauthier, Magali; Carney, Thomas J.; Grimaud, Alexis; Giordano, Livia; Pour, Nir; Chang, Hao-Hsun; Fenning, David P.; Lux, Simon F.; Paschos, Odysseas; Bauer, Christoph; Maglia, Filippo; Lupart, Saskia; Lamp, Peter; Shao-Horn, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4653-4672</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCLCD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1948-7185</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Understanding reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface (EEI) is essential to developing strategies to enhance cycle life and safety of lithium batteries.  Despite research in the past four decades, there is still limited understanding by what means different components are formed at the EEI and how they affect EEI layer properties.  Findings used to establish the well-known mosaic structure model are reviewed for the EEI (often referred to as solid electrolyte interphase or SEI) on neg. electrodes including lithium, graphite, tin, and silicon.  Much less understanding exists for EEI layers for pos. electrodes.  High-capacity Li-rich layered oxides yLi2-xMnO3·(1-y)Li1-xMO2, which can generate highly reactive species toward the electrolyte via oxygen anion redox, highlight the crit. need to understand reactions with the electrolyte and EEI layers for advanced pos. electrodes.  Recent advances in in situ characterization of well-defined electrode surfaces can provide mechanistic insights and strategies to tailor EEI layer compn. and properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoiaheBpm6thrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhfRuroWm4idg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslCisr3J&amp;md5=467ef5262b1622195d76dbf02765c5b3</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i17">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b05326" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b05326</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of pristine P2-NaNiMnO with its corresponding Rietveld refinement results. SEM/EDX images of the coated and uncoated P2-NaNiMnO cathode (FS2). Focused ion beam sample preparation to determine the ALD coating thickness. EIS of the three electrode cell showing that the cathode impedance can effectively be isolated. XPS spectra of Al 2p, Ni 2p, and Mn 2p. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b05326/suppl_file/am7b05326_si_001.pdf">am7b05326_si_001.pdf (956.32 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="90da8d5b1802b325-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="90da8d5b1802b325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8d5b1802b325-SJC">
"use strict"

	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da8d5b1802b325-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });

                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.

        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
